### Introduction

Urban planning and design are crucial components of urban governance. They involve the process of creating and managing the physical environment of a city, including its buildings, streets, parks, and public spaces. This chapter will provide a comprehensive guide to understanding and implementing effective urban planning and design strategies.

The chapter will begin by exploring the concept of urban planning and design, discussing their importance in creating livable, sustainable, and functional cities. It will then delve into the various aspects of urban planning and design, including land use planning, transportation planning, and public space design. Each of these aspects will be examined in detail, with a focus on their role in shaping the character and functionality of a city.

The chapter will also discuss the challenges and opportunities associated with urban planning and design. These include issues related to equity, sustainability, and community engagement, as well as opportunities for innovation and creativity in urban design. The chapter will provide practical strategies and examples to help readers navigate these challenges and opportunities.

Finally, the chapter will explore the role of urban planning and design in urban governance. It will discuss how urban planning and design can be used as tools for achieving broader urban policy objectives, such as promoting economic development, improving public health, and enhancing social equity. It will also examine the relationship between urban planning and design and other aspects of urban governance, such as policy-making, implementation, and evaluation.

In summary, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to urban planning and design, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools they need to create vibrant, livable, and sustainable cities. It is hoped that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for urban planners, designers, policymakers, and anyone else interested in the future of our cities.




### Subsection: 11.1a Definition of Urban Planning

Urban planning is a multidisciplinary field that involves the creation and management of the physical environment of a city. It is a process that guides the orderly development of urban areas, taking into account the needs and aspirations of the community, as well as the physical, social, and economic characteristics of the area. Urban planning is a crucial component of urban governance, as it helps to shape the character and functionality of a city, and can have a significant impact on the quality of life of its residents.

Urban planning is a complex and dynamic process that involves a wide range of stakeholders, including government agencies, urban planners, architects, engineers, community groups, and private developers. It is a process that is shaped by a variety of factors, including economic, social, political, and environmental considerations. The goal of urban planning is to create a city that is livable, sustainable, and functional, while also meeting the needs and aspirations of its residents.

Urban planning is not a linear process, but rather a cyclical one. It involves a series of steps, including analysis, planning, implementation, and evaluation. Each of these steps is crucial to the success of the planning process, and they are often iterative, meaning that they may need to be revisited and revised as the planning process progresses.

The field of urban planning has evolved significantly over time. In the early 20th century, urban planning was primarily focused on the physical layout of cities, with a focus on efficiency and sanitation. However, as the negative impacts of this approach became apparent, urban planning shifted its focus to the social and environmental bottom-lines, with a focus on improving the health and well-being of people while maintaining sustainability standards.

Today, urban planning is guided by a variety of principles and theories, including the principles of sustainability, equity, and community engagement. These principles help to guide the planning process, ensuring that it is responsive to the needs and aspirations of the community, and that it is carried out in a manner that is equitable, sustainable, and inclusive.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the principles and theories that guide urban planning, as well as the various aspects of urban planning and design, including land use planning, transportation planning, and public space design. We will also explore the challenges and opportunities associated with urban planning and design, and discuss practical strategies and examples to help readers navigate these challenges and opportunities.




### Subsection: 11.1b Role of Planning in Urban Governance

Urban planning plays a crucial role in urban governance. It is the process by which the physical environment of a city is shaped and managed, and it is a key factor in determining the quality of life for its residents. Urban planning is not just about creating a visually appealing city; it is also about addressing the complex and interconnected issues of economic development, social equity, and environmental sustainability.

#### 11.1b.1 Urban Planning and Economic Development

Economic development is a key aspect of urban governance. It involves creating an environment that fosters job creation, business growth, and increased wealth. Urban planning plays a critical role in this process by guiding the development of infrastructure, land use, and transportation systems that support economic activity. For example, a well-planned transportation system can reduce travel times and costs for businesses, making a city more attractive for investment. Similarly, a well-designed land use plan can create opportunities for mixed-use development, which can stimulate economic activity and increase property values.

#### 11.1b.2 Urban Planning and Social Equity

Social equity is another important aspect of urban governance. It involves ensuring that all residents have equal access to opportunities and resources, regardless of their socio-economic status. Urban planning can help promote social equity by creating inclusive and accessible public spaces, providing affordable housing options, and ensuring that all neighborhoods have access to essential services such as schools, healthcare, and public transportation.

#### 11.1b.3 Urban Planning and Environmental Sustainability

Environmental sustainability is a critical consideration in urban governance. It involves managing the use of resources and the impact of urban development on the environment in a way that meets the needs of the present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs. Urban planning plays a key role in this by guiding the development of sustainable infrastructure and land use patterns, promoting green building practices, and implementing policies to reduce waste and pollution.

In conclusion, urban planning is a vital component of urban governance. It is the process by which the physical environment of a city is shaped and managed, and it plays a crucial role in promoting economic development, social equity, and environmental sustainability. As such, it is essential for urban planners to work closely with other stakeholders, including government agencies, community groups, and private developers, to create a city that is livable, sustainable, and functional for all its residents.




### Subsection: 11.1c Case Studies of Urban Planning

In this section, we will explore some case studies of urban planning to gain a deeper understanding of how urban planning principles are applied in real-world scenarios. These case studies will provide practical examples of how urban planning can address the complex and interconnected issues of economic development, social equity, and environmental sustainability.

#### 11.1c.1 Curitiba, Brazil

Curitiba, Brazil, is often cited as a model for sustainable urban planning. The city has implemented a comprehensive public transportation system, including bus rapid transit and a network of bike lanes, which has reduced traffic congestion and air pollution. Curitiba's land use planning has also been innovative, with a focus on preserving green spaces and promoting mixed-use development. This has helped to create a high quality of life for its residents and has attracted businesses and tourists.

#### 11.1c.2 Portland, Oregon, USA

Portland, Oregon, is another example of a city that has successfully implemented urban planning principles. The city has a strong commitment to social equity, with policies in place to address issues of housing affordability and access to public services. Portland's urban planning has also been environmentally sustainable, with a focus on reducing energy consumption and promoting green building practices. The city has also implemented a comprehensive transportation plan, including a light rail system and a network of bike lanes, to reduce reliance on cars and promote sustainable transportation options.

#### 11.1c.3 Singapore

Singapore is a small island city-state that has achieved remarkable economic development while maintaining a high quality of life for its residents. The city's urban planning has been instrumental in this success. Singapore has a well-planned and efficient transportation system, with a comprehensive network of roads, public transportation options, and a port that handles a significant portion of the world's shipping traffic. The city's land use planning has also been strategic, with a focus on preserving green spaces and promoting mixed-use development. This has helped to create a vibrant and livable city.

These case studies demonstrate the importance of urban planning principles in creating sustainable and livable cities. They also highlight the role of urban planning in addressing the complex and interconnected issues of economic development, social equity, and environmental sustainability.




### Subsection: 11.2a Role of Design in Urban Governance

Urban design plays a crucial role in urban governance, as it is the process of shaping the physical features of a city to create a functional and aesthetically pleasing environment for its inhabitants. It involves the planning and design of public spaces, buildings, and infrastructure, and is guided by principles of sustainability, livability, and inclusivity.

#### 11.2a.1 The Politics of Design

The politics of design is a critical aspect of urban governance. As Kensing and Blomberg (1998) note, the introduction of new frameworks such as system design has raised concerns about power dynamics within the workspace. System design, which involves the automation of tasks, has been met with resistance from unions and workers who fear the loss of their involvement in production and ownership over their work situation. This highlights the importance of considering the political implications of design decisions in urban governance.

#### 11.2a.2 The Nature of Participation

Participation is another key aspect of urban design and governance. Major international organizations such as Project for Public Spaces (PPS) advocate for rigorous participation in the design and creation of place, believing that it is the essential ingredient for successful environments. PPS creates a platform for the community to participate and co-design new areas, which reflects their intimate knowledge and provides insights that independent design professionals may not have. This successfully engages with the ultimate goal of creating a livable and sustainable city for all its inhabitants.

#### 11.2a.3 Methods, Tools, and Techniques for Carrying Out Design Projects

The methods, tools, and techniques used in urban design are also important aspects of urban governance. These include participatory design, which involves the active involvement of the community in the design process, and place performance evaluation, a method used by PPS to evaluate the performance of public spaces. These tools and techniques not only help in creating functional and aesthetically pleasing environments, but also promote social equity and inclusivity, which are key principles of urban governance.

In conclusion, urban design plays a crucial role in urban governance. It involves the shaping of the physical features of a city, and is guided by principles of sustainability, livability, and inclusivity. The politics of design, the nature of participation, and the methods, tools, and techniques used in design projects are all important aspects of urban governance.




### Subsection: 11.2b Strategies for Designing Public Spaces

Public spaces are the heart of any city, providing a place for people to gather, interact, and enjoy the urban environment. The design of these spaces is a critical aspect of urban governance, as it directly impacts the quality of life for the city's inhabitants. In this section, we will explore some strategies for designing public spaces that are both functional and aesthetically pleasing.

#### 11.2b.1 The Role of Public Spaces in Urban Design

Public spaces are not just empty areas between buildings. They are integral parts of the urban fabric, contributing to the overall character and livability of the city. As noted by the Project for Public Spaces (PPS), public spaces are the "living rooms" of the city, where people come together to interact, relax, and enjoy the urban environment. Therefore, the design of these spaces should be guided by the principle of creating a place that people want to be in.

#### 11.2b.2 Strategies for Designing Public Spaces

There are several strategies that can be used to design effective public spaces. These include:

- **Observation and Analysis**: As noted by PPS, observation is key to understanding how a public space is used and what it needs. This involves observing the activities that take place in the space, the types of people who use it, and the interactions that occur. This information can then be used to inform the design of the space.

- **Participatory Design**: Involving the community in the design process can lead to more successful outcomes. As PPS notes, the community often has valuable insights into how the space should function and what it should include. This can be achieved through community workshops, design charrettes, and other participatory methods.

- **Multi-Modal Design**: Public spaces should be designed to accommodate a variety of activities and modes of transportation. This includes pedestrian-friendly design, as well as accommodations for cyclists, public transit, and private vehicles.

- **Sustainability**: Public spaces should be designed with sustainability in mind. This includes using sustainable materials, incorporating green infrastructure, and designing for energy efficiency.

- **Adaptability and Flexibility**: Public spaces should be designed to be adaptable and flexible, allowing them to evolve and respond to changing needs and circumstances. This can be achieved through modular design, flexible layouts, and the use of temporary or movable elements.

#### 11.2b.3 Case Studies of Successful Public Space Design

There are many examples of successful public space design around the world. These include the High Line in New York City, the Olympic Village in Barcelona, and the Waterfront Promenade in Vancouver. Each of these spaces has its own unique characteristics and design strategies, but they all share a common goal: to create a vibrant, livable, and sustainable public space for the community.

In conclusion, the design of public spaces is a critical aspect of urban governance. By using strategies such as observation, participatory design, multi-modal design, sustainability, and adaptability, and by learning from successful case studies, urban planners and designers can create public spaces that are both functional and aesthetically pleasing, contributing to the overall livability and quality of life in the city.




#### 11.2c Case Studies of Urban Design

In this section, we will explore some case studies of urban design to further illustrate the principles and strategies discussed in the previous sections. These case studies will provide real-world examples of how urban design can be used to create vibrant and livable cities.

##### 11.2c.1 The High Line, New York City

The High Line is a 1.45-mile-long elevated linear park built on a former freight rail line in New York City. The project transformed an abandoned and derelict railway into a vibrant public space that has become a popular tourist attraction and a beloved local resource. The design of the High Line was guided by the principles of sustainability, community engagement, and multi-modal design. The project incorporated green infrastructure to manage stormwater, and the design process involved extensive community engagement, including a design competition and public workshops. The High Line is a prime example of how urban design can transform a neglected space into a valuable public resource.

##### 11.2c.2 The Beltline, Atlanta

The Beltline is a 22-mile-long multi-use trail and greenway that connects 45 neighborhoods in Atlanta. The project is guided by a vision of creating a more livable, equitable, and sustainable city. The design of the Beltline is informed by a comprehensive planning process that includes community engagement, environmental analysis, and economic analysis. The project also incorporates principles of sustainability, including the use of green infrastructure and a focus on energy efficiency. The Beltline is a model of how urban design can be used to create a more sustainable and equitable city.

##### 11.2c.3 The Zuidas District, Amsterdam

The Zuidas District is a new urban development in Amsterdam that will be home to 75,000 residents and 35,000 jobs. The project is guided by a vision of creating a sustainable and livable city center. The design of the Zuidas District is informed by a comprehensive planning process that includes environmental analysis, economic analysis, and community engagement. The project incorporates principles of sustainability, including the use of green infrastructure and a focus on energy efficiency. The Zuidas District is a prime example of how urban design can be used to create a sustainable and livable city.

These case studies illustrate the principles and strategies of urban design in action. They show how urban design can be used to create vibrant, sustainable, and livable cities. They also highlight the importance of observation, analysis, and community engagement in the design process.




### Subsection: 11.3a Role of Land Use in Urban Governance

Land use plays a crucial role in urban governance, as it is the foundation upon which all other aspects of urban planning and design are built. It involves the management and modification of natural environment or wilderness into built environment such as settlements and semi-natural habitats such as arable fields, pastures, and managed woods. Land use by humans has a long history, first emerging more than 10,000 years ago. It has been defined as "the purposes and activities through which people interact with land and terrestrial ecosystems" and as "the total of arrangements, activities, and inputs that people undertake in a certain land type." Land use is one of the most important drivers of global environmental change.

## History

Human tribes since prehistory have segregated land into territories to control the use of land. Today, the total arable land is 10.7% of the land surface, with 1.3% being permanent cropland.

## Regulation

Land use practices vary considerably across the world. The United Nations' Food and Agriculture Organization Water Development Division explains that "Land use concerns the products and/or benefits obtained from use of the land as well as the land management actions (activities) carried out by humans to produce those products and benefits." As of the early 1990s, about 13% of the Earth was considered arable land, with 26% in pasture, 32% forests and woodland, and 1.5% urban areas. Land change modeling can be used to predict and assess future shifts in land use.

As Albert Guttenberg (1959) wrote many years ago, "'Land use' is a key term in the language of city planning." Commonly, political jurisdictions will undertake land-use planning and regulate the use of land in an attempt to avoid land-use conflicts. Land use plans are implemented through land division and use ordinances and regulations, such as zoning regulations. Management consulting firms and non-governmental organizations will frequently seek to influence these regulations before they are codified.




### Subsection: 11.3b Impact of Zoning on Cities

Zoning, as a tool for urban planning and design, has a significant impact on cities. It is a regulatory process that divides a municipality into different zones, each with its own set of permitted land uses and development standards. Zoning is used to guide development and ensure that it aligns with the community's vision and goals. It is a crucial aspect of urban governance, as it helps to shape the physical form and character of a city.

#### 11.3b.1 Zoning and Smart Growth

Zoning plays a crucial role in achieving smart growth objectives. Smart growth is a planning and design approach that aims to create sustainable, walkable, and transit-friendly communities. Zoning ordinances are often modified to increase the density of development and redevelopment allowed in or near existing towns and neighborhoods, and to restrict new development in outlying or environmentally sensitive areas. This helps to create compact, walkable, and transit-friendly communities, which are key principles of smart growth.

Additional density incentives can be offered for development of brownfield and greyfield land or for providing amenities such as parks and open space. This not only encourages development in areas that are already built up, but also helps to create more livable and sustainable communities. Zoning ordinances typically include minimum parking requirements. Reductions in or elimination of parking minimums or imposition of parking maximums can also reduce the amount of parking built with new development, increasing land available for parks and other community amenities.

#### 11.3b.2 Zoning and Urban Growth Boundaries

Related to zoning ordinances, an urban growth boundary (UGB) is a tool used in some U.S. cities to contain high density development to certain areas. The first urban growth boundary in the United States was established in 1958 in Kentucky. Subsequently, urban growth boundaries were established in Oregon in the 1970s and Florida in the 1980s. Some believe that UGBs contributed to the escalation of housing prices from 2000 to 2006, as they limited the supply of developable land. However, this is not completely substantiated because prices continued to rise even after municipalities expanded their growth boundaries.

#### 11.3b.3 Zoning and Transfer of Development Rights

Transfer of development rights (TDR) systems are intended to allow property owners in areas deemed desirable for growth (such as infill and brownfield sites) to purchase the right to build at higher densities from owners of properties in areas deemed undesirable for growth such as environmental lands, farmlands or lands outside of an urban growth boundary. TDR programs have been implemented in over 200 U.S. cities and have been successful in preserving open space and promoting compact, walkable, and transit-friendly development.

In conclusion, zoning plays a crucial role in shaping cities. It helps to guide development, create compact, walkable, and transit-friendly communities, and preserve open space. However, it is important to ensure that zoning is implemented in a fair and equitable manner, and that it aligns with the community's vision and goals.




### Subsection: 11.3c Case Studies of Land Use and Zoning

In this section, we will explore some case studies of land use and zoning to gain a deeper understanding of how these tools are applied in real-world scenarios. These case studies will provide practical examples and insights into the challenges and benefits of land use and zoning.

#### 11.3c.1 Case Study 1: Smart Growth in Portland, Oregon

Portland, Oregon, is often cited as a model for smart growth and sustainable urban development. The city has implemented a comprehensive land use and zoning system that encourages compact, walkable, and transit-friendly communities. The city's urban growth boundary (UGB) has been instrumental in guiding development and preserving open space.

The city's zoning ordinances have been modified to increase the density of development and redevelopment allowed in or near existing towns and neighborhoods. This has helped to create compact, walkable, and transit-friendly communities. Additionally, the city offers density incentives for development of brownfield and greyfield land, as well as for providing amenities such as parks and open space.

#### 11.3c.2 Case Study 2: Transfer of Development Rights in San Francisco, California

San Francisco, California, has implemented a transfer of development rights (TDR) system to address the challenges of development in environmentally sensitive areas. The city's TDR program allows property owners in areas deemed desirable for growth to purchase the right to build at higher densities from owners of properties in areas deemed undesirable for growth.

This system has helped to preserve open space and environmentally sensitive areas, while also promoting development in areas that are already built up. It has also provided an incentive for developers to redevelop brownfield and greyfield sites, which can help to revitalize these areas and create more livable communities.

#### 11.3c.3 Case Study 3: Urban Growth Boundary in Lexington, Kentucky

Lexington, Kentucky, was one of the first cities in the United States to establish an urban growth boundary (UGB). The city's UGB has been instrumental in guiding development and preserving open space. The city's zoning ordinances have also been modified to encourage compact, walkable, and transit-friendly communities.

The city's UGB has helped to contain high density development to certain areas, while also promoting development in areas that are already built up. This has helped to create more livable and sustainable communities. The city's zoning ordinances have also been instrumental in achieving smart growth objectives, such as promoting compact, walkable, and transit-friendly communities.




### Subsection: 11.4a Role of Redevelopment in Urban Governance

Urban redevelopment plays a crucial role in urban governance, particularly in the context of sustainable development and nation-building. It is a process that involves the revitalization of existing urban areas, often with the aim of creating more livable, sustainable, and economically vibrant communities. This section will explore the role of redevelopment in urban governance, with a focus on sustainable redevelopment and its significance in post-disaster countries.

#### 11.4a.1 Sustainable Redevelopment

Sustainable redevelopment is a doctrine that emphasizes the recreation of a socio-economic position while upholding sustainable and environmental practices. It is based on the principles of economics within environmentalism, and it seeks to create systems that are less energy-intensive, less wasteful, and generally less expensive to maintain in the long run. By redeveloping sustainably, long-term energy efficiencies can be realized, coupled with highly reduced greenhouse gas emissions.

Within the context of nation-building, sustainable redevelopment can take on a greater significance. Post-disaster countries, for instance, are in a unique situation to deal with these systems of ideals. The idea behind initiating post-trauma sustainable redevelopment is that the underlying infrastructure is destroyed or damaged, providing an opportunity to realize the opportunities inherent to the situation.

#### 11.4a.2 Redevelopment and Post-Disaster Countries

In post-disaster countries, the process of redevelopment can be particularly challenging and complex. However, it also presents a unique opportunity to create more sustainable and resilient communities. The Kyoto Protocol's 12th Article, which speaks to "flexible mechanisms," can be particularly relevant in this context. These mechanisms allow countries to lower their overall emissions through the most economical means, respectively, of course, to that country. Theoretically, these mechanisms could be directed towards post-traumatic redevelopment.

#### 11.4a.3 Redevelopment and Urban Governance

Redevelopment plays a crucial role in urban governance, particularly in the context of sustainable development and nation-building. It is a process that involves the revitalization of existing urban areas, often with the aim of creating more livable, sustainable, and economically vibrant communities. By incorporating principles of sustainable redevelopment, urban governance can help to create more resilient and environmentally friendly cities.

In the next section, we will explore some case studies of urban redevelopment to gain a deeper understanding of how these principles are applied in real-world scenarios.
